        };

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202408962')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4c2ec0da72b3427b93d8d8434ebed138">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '41a89587-160c-4cff-9644-dbef430bb523'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d49766634" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Developing advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is a frontier and hotspot research for clean and sustainable electricity energy storage and conversion. However, it is still challenging to obtain anode materials with high stability, multiple active sites, and high conductivity. In this paper, an advanced metal–organic framework (MOF)-based anode material (Co-HITP), which integrates multiple advantages into one, is successfully synthesized through a sodiation-stimulated in situ reconstruction. The Co-HITP possesses a stable single-crystal structure, expanded spacing layers, abundant sites, and good conductivity, and exhibits superior properties with ultra-long cycling stability (ultralow decay rate ≈0.001% per cycle after 15&nbsp;000 cycles at 8&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), high reversible capacity (450.1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and excellent rate performance. Both experimental results and theoretical calculation reveal that the anode remains a stable structure after long cycling, attributed to the single-crystal structure with d-<i>π</i> hybridization and <i>π</i>–<i>π</i> interaction, and the minimum structural unit stores 6Na<sup>+</sup> ions of C═N sites and 8Na<sup>+</sup> ions of aromatic rings. This discovery is significant for developing advanced anode materials with integrated advantages for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202408962-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202408962-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) possessing advantages of rich sodium abundance and less performance drop at low-temperature operation (for example below −20&nbsp;°C) are expected to be practically used in clean and sustainable energy storage and conversion systems including portable electronics, electric vehicles, and stationary power stations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0001_R_d49766614e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0005_R_d49766614e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The structures and compositions of SIBs anodes are identified to be one of the limiting factors determining the performance of batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0006_R_d49766614e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A high-performance anode material should have characteristics of ample active sites to provide high reversible specific capacity, excellent electron and ion transport properties to guarantee excellent rate performance, and high robustness to possess long cycle life. At present, commonly used anode materials for SIBs are insertion-type materials, alloy-type materials, conversion-type materials, and organic materials. For insertion-type materials, hard carbon materials are the most potential anode for commercial SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0007_R_d49766614e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the insufficient capacity due to limited active sites and the low first Coulombic efficiency (CE) of hard carbon materials has become the important factor affecting their practical application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0008_R_d49766614e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For alloy-type and conversion-type materials, show high theoretical capacities, while their volume change is large during the charge/discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0009_R_d49766614e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0010_R_d49766614e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For organic materials, they have a wide range of sources, low-cost, diverse structures, and multi-electron reactions for high electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0011_R_d49766614e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, organic materials have low electronic conductivities and poor stability in organic solvents. Therefore, developing new and high-performance anode materials that can integrate multiple structures and functions into one through expanding layer spacing, creating abundant active sites, increasing conductivity, and achieving robust structures/compositions is necessary for practicable SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Concerning the development of new SIB anodes, metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) materials have been explored in most recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0012_R_d49766614e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Joule">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MOFs are assembled by metal ions or clusters and organic ligands, which can provide multifold active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0013_R_d49766614e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cheng et&nbsp;al. reported a MOF-177-based anode material of metal-ion battery in 2006.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0014_R_d49766614e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Power Sources">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since then, many researches have been carried out to develop a variety of MOFs-based materials for Li/Na-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0015_R_d49766614e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0016_R_d49766614e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although MOFs-based electrode materials can show unique structural advantages in electrochemical energy storage devices, most of them have insufficient electronic conductivities and structural stability, which could limit their redox activities of active sites. As identified, the metal nodes and organic ligands have low orbital overlapping, resulting in intrinsic low conductivity and slow electron transport in the active sites of MOFs during the actual electrochemical reactions of SIBs. In addition, most MOFs are unstable in some environments (such as acidic, alkaline, organic solvents, or pressurized conditions), resulting in reduced reactivity of the active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0017_R_d49766614e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0018_R_d49766614e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, enhancing the intrinsic structural stability and increasing conductivity of MOFs-based anodes are needed to improve their electrochemical properties. Normally, high-temperature calcination is often used to increase the electrical conductivity and structural stability of MOFs. However, high temperatures often make MOFs become derivatives.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0019_R_d49766614e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0020_R_d49766614e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with the original MOFs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0021_R_d49766614e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it is difficult for the derivatives to maintain the frame structure of the original MOFs. The specific surface area of the derivatives is also reduced due to their destroyed pore structure and decreased number of active sites. The combination of MOFs with conductive carbon materials or with other coating materials is also a popular method to increase the electrical conductivity and stability of the anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0022_R_d49766614e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> But this method also has some drawbacks. On the one hand, the additional material can affect the exposure of MOF's active sites; on the other hand, when MOFs are combined with external materials, there is a different orientation interface or a low recombination degree, which can affect the effective transport of electrons and ions during the battery cycling. Therefore, further improving the stability, active sites, and conductivities of MOFs-based SIB anodes is still necessary. For this, the construction of d-<i>π</i> conjugated 2D conductive MOFs structures<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0023_R_d49766614e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0024_R_d49766614e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with a high density of active sites and stable single-crystal structure seems an effective strategy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0025_R_d49766614e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0026_R_d49766614e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The d-<i>π</i> hybridization orbitals of metal ions and ligands allow electrons to delocalize throughout the system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0027_R_d49766614e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The large electron delocalization system can make the redox reaction-generated free radicals exist stably, effectively preventing the self-polymerization of free radicals, and providing a stable framework for realizing reversible charge/discharge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0028_R_d49766614e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These 2D planes can be further stacked along the c-axis to get a porous structure with 1D open channels by <i>π</i>–<i>π</i> interaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0025_R_d49766614e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0026_R_d49766614e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 2D planes and 1D open channels can realize rapid transport of electrons both in-plane and out-of-plane, which gives the material a high intrinsic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0029_R_d49766614e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high intrinsic conductivity can make electrons transport to the redox active sites quickly, which then improves the reactivity of the active sites. The open 1D pores are found to store more sodium ions and provide channels for ion transport for improving the reaction kinetics at the active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0030_R_d49766614e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this work, a conductive single-crystal 2D nanoribbon MOF (named Co-HITP) for SIB anodes is successfully synthesized through in situ electrochemical reconstruction of an amorphous MOF precursor (Co-HITP-P) constructed via the ligand HITP and Co<sup>2+</sup> salt. The quadrangular coordination structure of Co ions and <i>π</i>-conjugated organic ligands expands along the ab plane to form a 2D planar network structure with a graphene-like structure. The samples obtained by in situ reconstruction have better compatibility with the electrolyte and better fit with copper foil. In addition, the Co-HITP obtained through in situ electrochemical reconstruction has excellent intrinsic structural stability and electrical conductivity. The in situ reconstructed conductive single-crystal 2D nanoribbon Co-HITP can provide an expanded spacing layer and abundant sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage to deliver high capacity (366.1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and the stable structure can guarantee ultra-long cycling stability (ultralow decay rate ≈0.001% per cycle after 15&nbsp;000 cycles at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and superior rate performance. The charged/discharged mechanism reveals that the anode can remain a stable structure after long cycling, attributed to the stable single-crystal structure with d-<i>π</i> hybridization and <i>π</i>–<i>π</i> interaction, and the minimum structural unit can store 14Na<sup>+</sup> ions to provide a high capacity. The synthesis strategy developed in this study can offer guidance for the development of novel SIB anode materials with ultra-high cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202408962-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202408962-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408962-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202408962-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>First, an amorphous MOF precursor (Co-HITP-P) was synthesized. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0001">1a</a>, HITP (2,3,6,7,10,11-hexaminotriphenylene) ligand and Co<sup>2+</sup> ion are self-assembled to generate Co-HITP-P. The Co-HITP-P sample is composed of irregular nanosheets and presents a large number of micro-folds (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2a–c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). There are no obvious lattice fringes in the high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HR-TEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2d</a>) and diffraction rings in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) image of Co-HITP-P. Moreover, no diffraction peaks can be seen (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). These results indicate that the Co-HITP-P is amorphous. Meanwhile, the Co-HITP-P has a large surface area (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), which is beneficial for achieving a large surface of the target sample. Then, the Co-HITP-P is in situ converted to 2D nanoribbon Co-HITP during the electrochemical process. The <i>π</i>-d bond is formed between the -NH<sub>2</sub> groups of the organic ligand and the metal center, which is favorable for improving the electrical conductivity and stability of the MOFs. In addition, the 2D nanoribbon is assembled by many nanosheets with expanded layer spacing, which can provide enough space for Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408962-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/86d4c9b2-4c93-45a9-9a4a-052217bd7037/adfm202408962-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/86d4c9b2-4c93-45a9-9a4a-052217bd7037/adfm202408962-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/327ca7f2-1ce3-44a9-b537-0e988b8ccc24/adfm202408962-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/86d4c9b2-4c93-45a9-9a4a-052217bd7037/adfm202408962-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408962-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408962" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthetic diagram of the Co-HITP MOF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408962-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/117b74ae-e0a2-48f0-b967-d63befff1992/adfm202408962-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/117b74ae-e0a2-48f0-b967-d63befff1992/adfm202408962-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2d76a6cd-c891-4173-a041-06c8a705f075/adfm202408962-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/117b74ae-e0a2-48f0-b967-d63befff1992/adfm202408962-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408962-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408962" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) FESEM images of different magnifications of the Co-HITP-P. c) TEM images and d) HR-TEM images of the Co-HITP-P (inset: the SAED image). e–h) FESEM image of the Co-HITP electrode after 1st, 2nd, 3rd and 9999th cycles. The Co-HITP after 9999th cycles: i–k) TEM images at different magnifications. l) HR-TEM and SEAD (inset). m) HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding elemental mappings.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>After the charge/discharge process, the amorphous MOF precursor changes into crystal MOF, and the morphology of the Co-HITP-P is also changed greatly. In the process of sodium storage, the Co-HITP-P shows a transition from the irregular nanosheet precursor (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2b,c</a>) to grass-like nanoribbon (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2e–h</a>). Interestingly, the morphology reconstruction occurs only during the charge/discharge processes. The field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) images display the morphology of anode material in ink after grinding (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information), the ink soaked in electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information), and the ink further coated on copper foil (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c</a>, Supporting Information) don't transfer into grass-like nanoribbon. After the charge/discharge process, the grass-like nanoribbon appears (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5d</a>, Supporting Information). To deeply study the transformation process of the Co-HITP sample, the morphology changes of Co-HITP after the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 9999th charge/discharge cycles are characterized (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2e–m</a>). The morphology has the biggest change during the 1st cycle. During the 1st cycle, the nanoribbon Co-HITP appears, but not completely (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2e</a>). At the state of 1st discharge to 0.5&nbsp;V, nanoribbons appear partly (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequently, these nanoribbons gradually increase in size, and become longer and thinner (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b–d</a>, Supporting Information). The FESEM images after the 2nd and 3rd cycles of the Co-HITP show that the morphology has completely changed into grass-like nanoribbon (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2f,g</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6e,f</a>, Supporting Information). For the 3rd cycle, these nanoribbons are uniform, and there are no differences in the charging process (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6e</a>, Supporting Information) and the discharging process (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6f</a>, Supporting Information). The morphology of the Co-HITP still keeps a uniform grass-like nanoribbon structure after 9999th cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2h–j</a>). Further characterization reveals that the Co-HITP nanoribbons formed in situ on the anodes show layered and single-crystal structures. The magnified TEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2k</a>) shows that these nanoribbons are assembled by graphene-like thin nanosheets with large layer spacing, which could provide large space for Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage and transport without deformation. Single lattice fringe in HR-TEM and uniform symmetrical diffraction spots in SEAD indicate that the anode sample has a monocrystal structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2l</a>). The lattice spacing of 0.47&nbsp;nm is indexed to the (400) plane. The XRD result also suggests that the crystallization strength of the Co-HITP becomes strong (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the transfer of amorphous Co-HITP-P to crystal form Co-HITP. The peaks that appear at 4.7°, 9.5°, and 27.2° can be indexed to (100), (200), and (001) planes, respectively. In addition, high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) and the corresponding elemental mappings show Co, C, and N elements are homogeneously dispersed in the Co-HITP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0002">2m</a>). In conclusion, the large layer spacing, nanosheets, and single-crystal structure can expose more active sites, store more Na<sup>+</sup> ions and provide a more stable structure.</p>
            
            <p>The valence bonds and coordination structure of the Co-HITP-P precursor and the Co-HITP were investigated by Fourier-transform infrared (FT-IR) spectroscopy, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), and X-ray absorption fine structure (XAFS). As shown in FT-IR spectra (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3a</a>), compared with the ligand HITP, the stretching vibration peaks of ─NH<sub>2</sub> at 3400 and 3336 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the Co-HITP-P and the Co-HITP samples disappear due to the coordination between N and Co, while the stretching vibration and in-plane bending signal peaks of ─NH appear at ≈3428 and 1040 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which confirms the successful synthesis of the Co-HITP-P and the Co-HITP MOF. XPS was also measured to study the chemical composition, valence bond, and the element valence of the Co-HITP. The results of XPS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3b,c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) show that there are no differences in chemical composition and the element valence of the Co-HITP-P and Co-HITP. In the high-resolution Co 2p XPS spectrum of the Co-HITP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3b</a>), the peaks at 781.3 and 797.1&nbsp;eV correspond to Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, which can be attributed to Co-N<sub>x</sub>. The peaks at 787.4 and 803.2&nbsp;eV belong to the satellite peaks of Co. The high-resolution N 1s XPS spectrum can be deconvoluted in three peaks at 398.5&nbsp;eV (C═N), 399.6&nbsp;eV (C─N), and 400.5&nbsp;eV (N─H)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0031_R_d49766614e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Dalton Trans.">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0032_R_d49766614e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3c</a>). XAFS analyses were performed to illustrate the structure of samples at the atomic level (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3d–f</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3d</a>, the Co K-edge of the Co-HITP in the X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) is located between the absorption edge of Co foil and CoO, indicating that the Co valence state in the Co-HITP is between Co<sup>0</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup>, which is similar to that of CoPc. The Fourier-transformed extended X-ray absorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) spectrum of the Co-HITP is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3e</a>. The coordination peak near 1.5&nbsp;Å corresponds to the Co–N distance in the first shell. The fitting analysis of the EXAFS curve shows that the Co–N coordination number is ≈4. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0003">3f</a>, the maximum wavelet transform intensity near 1.5 Å in R-space is related to the coordination of Co-N. The coordination environment and coordination number have no changes in the Co-HITP-P and Co-HITP. Combined with the XPS and XAFS tests discussed above, the coordination of Co with N to form a Co-N<sub>4</sub> structure is confirmed.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408962-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97beae1a-8aaa-4ee1-9233-4df2972a8257/adfm202408962-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/97beae1a-8aaa-4ee1-9233-4df2972a8257/adfm202408962-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/01a9b076-53cc-4d70-8ab2-e89391f46477/adfm202408962-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97beae1a-8aaa-4ee1-9233-4df2972a8257/adfm202408962-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408962-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202408962-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The results of electrochemical performance indicate the Co-HITP electrode presents both excellent electrochemical activity and stability. The FT-IR spectra of the Co-HITP after soaking in the electrolyte for 24&nbsp;h have no change, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), indicating its feasibility to be used as the stable anode material of SIBs. The initial five Cyclic Voltammetry (CV) curves of the Co-HITP are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0004">4a</a>. A reduction peak at 1.0&nbsp;V appears in the 1st cathodic scan, which is attributed to the generation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. Except for the first cycle, the subsequent CV curves almost overlap, suggesting this Co-HITP anode can give excellent reversibility. A pair of peaks at 0.56&nbsp;V of the cathodic scans and 1.3&nbsp;V of the anodic scans can be attributed to the reversible redox processes between C═N and C─N, which can also be demonstrated by the following XPS tests. The discharge/charge curves of Co-HITP at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0004">4b</a>. The Co-HITP anode possesses an initial discharge capacity of 2019.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and a charge capacity of 576.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, with a low Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 28.5%. The irreversible loss of capacity is related to the generation of SEI film. The cycling performance of Co-HITP at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0004">4c</a>) shows the capacity remains 366.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles with a CE of 99.1%. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0004">4d</a>, a high capacity of up to 352&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0004">4e</a> shows that the Co-HITP can exhibit stable capacities of 640, 540, 460, 406, 357, 300, and 244&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, demonstrating its excellent rate performance. Furthermore, when the current density returns to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity of the Co-HITP anode is returned to 500&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating the exceptional tolerance for the repeated sodiation/desodiation processes. Besides, the Co-HITP electrode can keep a capacity of 192&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 15000 cycles at a high current density of 8.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity retention rate is up to 82.0%, suggesting its high cycle stability. Compared with other reported MOF electrodes for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0004">4f</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the Co-HITP electrode exhibits remarkable cycling stability, rate performance, and capacity. Especially, the cycling stability is better than other types of anodes reported in the literature (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). For the full cell performance (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) of this Co-HITP anode, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF) was used as the cathode to assemble a SIB full battery. The full battery expresses a good rate performance (discharge capacities of 252.7, 211.5, 194.2, 163.9, and 128.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively), a high capacity (initial discharge capacity of 197&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), and long cycling stability (discharge capacity of 160.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, and capacity retention rate of 81.5%).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408962-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/59df6b11-d7eb-4d54-9d19-0aaab1172028/adfm202408962-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/59df6b11-d7eb-4d54-9d19-0aaab1172028/adfm202408962-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d846ace8-9cdc-461f-8efe-5f322e50081d/adfm202408962-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/59df6b11-d7eb-4d54-9d19-0aaab1172028/adfm202408962-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408962-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202408962-sec-0050-title">2.3 Charge Storage Mechanisms</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical kinetics of the Co-HITP anode were further revealed. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0005">5a</a> shows the CV curves of the Co-HITP anode at different scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. It can be seen that with increasing sweep rates, the shapes of CV curves are nearly the same, suggesting that the redox reaction of the Co-HITP electrode is highly reversible. The relationship between peak currents (<i>i</i>) and scan rates (<i>v</i>) follows the equation: <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup>b</sup>, further written as: log(<i>i</i>) = <i>b</i>log(<i>v</i>) + log(<i>a</i>). Generally speaking, the value of b tends to be 0.5 indicating that the electrochemical behavior is dominated by diffusion. In contrast, it is capacitive-controlled when b is near to 1. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0005">5b</a> exhibits the values of b for two redox peaks (0.76 and 0.81, respectively), which implies that the charge storage of the Co-HITP anode has both diffusion and capacitive behavior. Furthermore, the contribution ratio of capacitive and diffusion behavior can be obtained by this equation: <i>i = k<sub>1</sub>v + k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup></i>. <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i> are the constants that can be deduced by fitting <i>iv<sup>−1/2</sup></i> versus <i>v<sup>1/2</sup></i>. The capacitive contribution is found to increase from 54.5% to 78.9% at different scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0005">5c</a>). The capacitance contribution ratio is ≈71.2% at 0.6&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0005">5d</a>). The Co-HITP-P can transform into the Co-HITP during the CV test. For the later cycles of CV, the Co-HITP-P and the Co-HITP are the one material. So, the values of b, and the contribution ratio of capacitive, and diffusion behavior of the Co-HITP-P anode are close to those of the Co-HITP anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0005">5e–g</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The excellent capacitance contribution rate of electrodes can accelerate electron and ion transport at a high scan rate, improving the reaction kinetics.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408962-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8479a5d3-6ba8-4d0f-bca2-dbc132e35852/adfm202408962-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8479a5d3-6ba8-4d0f-bca2-dbc132e35852/adfm202408962-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/35c45e47-5d66-4e99-94b8-3aed52ab98a8/adfm202408962-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8479a5d3-6ba8-4d0f-bca2-dbc132e35852/adfm202408962-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408962-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408962" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The Co-HITP anode: a) CV curves at different scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) The corresponding plots of log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>). c) Ratios of capacitive contribution at different scan rates. d) Capacitance and diffusion-controlled contribution at 0.6&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The Co-HITP-P electrode: e) The corresponding plots of log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>). f) Percentages of capacitive contribution at different scan rates. g) Capacitance and diffusion-controlled contribution at 0.6&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. h) Nyquist plot and i) corresponding Zre versus ω<sup>−1/2</sup> plots of the Co-HITP electrode and Co-HITP-P electrode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The reaction kinetics of the Co-HITP electrode were also evaluated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0005">5h,i</a>). The semicircles in the high-frequency region and straight lines in the low-frequency region are the charge-transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) and the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>, respectively. The value of R<sub>ct</sub> for the Co-HITP electrode is lower than that of the Co-HITP-P electrode. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics during discharge and charge were also evaluated through the Experimental Section (Supporting Information. The D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values of the Co-HITP and Co-HITP-P electrodes are 5.50 × 10<sup>−9</sup> and 3.77 × 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The kinetic studies indicate that the Co-HITP electrode has both faster electronic and charger transfer than the Co-HITP-P electrode. The fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics guarantee excellent rate performance of the Co-HITP anode.</p>
            
            <p>To further study the sodium storage mechanism of the Co-HITP anode, both XPS and FT-IR spectra were implemented. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0006">6b</a> shows the FT-IR spectra of the Co-HITP anode at different discharge/charge states in the 1st cycle. The peak of C═N at ≈1630&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> decreases with increasing C─N at 1425 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the discharge process. The reverse trend is found after being recharged, indicating the ligand has active sites to accept Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The XPS spectra of N 1s and C 1s (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0006">6c,d</a>) also confirm this mechanism. Compared with the pristine Co-HITP electrode, the C═N peak weakens, and the intensity of C–N peak becomes strong at the discharge state. The opposite process occurs in the charge state. The Co 2p XPS spectra of the Co-HITP at different charge/discharge states are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0006">6e</a>. Both peaks at 781.4&nbsp;eV (Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 797.2&nbsp;eV (Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) have no changes during the discharge and charge processes, suggesting that the metal center cobalt doesn't undergo conversion reaction and also doesn't participate in the sodium storage process. Meanwhile, the FT-IR and XPS of the stable Co-HITP in different states were tested. The change trend is consistent with that of the 1st circle sample.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408962-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/80e0ffdf-53d4-45da-a700-a934d1cf9d50/adfm202408962-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/80e0ffdf-53d4-45da-a700-a934d1cf9d50/adfm202408962-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5b4cf0af-0c81-4cb8-b4aa-a29231d69340/adfm202408962-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/80e0ffdf-53d4-45da-a700-a934d1cf9d50/adfm202408962-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408962-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408962" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Discharge/charge profiles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. b) FT-IR spectra. c) High-resolution XPS of N 1s. d) High-resolution XPS of C 1s. e) High-resolution XPS of Co 2p. f) HOMO and g) LUMO molecular orbital. h) ESP, and i) discharge/charge mechanism of the Co-HITP electrode.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0015" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0015_R_d49766614e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0035" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0035_R_d49766614e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem.">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0037" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0037_R_d49766614e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The theoretical calculations also show that the active sites are the ligands, observed by using the minimum structure unit of the Co-HITP. The energy difference value of HOMO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0006">6f</a>) and LUMO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0006">6g</a>) molecular orbitals is 1.13&nbsp;eV, which suggests good conductivity. In addition, N atoms possess 36% of the LUMO composition, indicating that N atoms are mainly responsible for storing Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The red and blue colors denote negative and positive electrostatic potential (ESP) values that correspond to nucleophilicity and electrophilicity, respectively. It can be seen that N atoms possess the largest negative values of ESP, indicating that N atoms have nucleophilicity to store Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The aromatic rings also have negative ESP values, which can insert Na<sup>+</sup> ions to form Na/C<sub>6</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0015_R_d49766614e1196" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0035_R_d49766614e1196" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem.">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202408962-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408962-bib-0037_R_d49766614e1196" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the theoretical calculations, the discharge and charge mechanisms are proposed as in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408962-fig-0006">6i</a>. During the discharge/charge processes, the minimum structural unit can store 6Na<sup>+</sup> ions of C═N active sites and insert 8Na<sup>+</sup> ions of aromatic rings, which can provide a theoretical capacity of 468.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202408962-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202408962-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, a conductive single-crystal 2D nanoribbon Co-HITP MOF is successfully synthesized through in situ sodiation-stimulated electrochemical reconstruction. The 2D nanoribbon is assembled by a graphene-like thin layer with large layer spacing. The conjugated single-crystal structure with high conductivity and the expanded layer spacing shows superior electrochemical performance (high initial charge capacity of 576.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, ultra-long cycling stability of ≈0.001% decay rate per cycle after 15000 cycles at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent rate performance). This study not only lays a foundation for studying the mechanism of sodium storage of MOF but also provides ideas for designing and developing new long-cycle electrode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
